margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;"><b>Anthem:</b> "<a href="/wiki/The_NATO_Hymn" title="The NATO Hymn">The NATO Hymn</a>"<div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;"><audio controls="" data-durationhint="86" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle='"The_NATO_Hymn",_performed_by_the_United_States_Air_Force_Band.mp3' id="mwe_player_0" preload="none" style="width:220px;" width="220"><source data-bandwidth="192041" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="MP3 source" data-title="Original MP3 file (192 kbps)" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/28/%22The_NATO_Hymn%22%2C_performed_by_the_United_States_Air_Force_Band.mp3" type="audio/mpeg"/></audio></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <b>North Atlantic Treaty Organization</b> (<b>NATO</b>, <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/eɪ/: 'a' in 'face'">eɪ</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/oʊ/: 'o' in 'code'">oʊ</span></span>/</a></span></span>; <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>: <i lang="fr">Organisation du traité de l'Atlantique nord</i>, <b><span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">OTAN</i></span></b>), also called the <b>North Atlantic Alliance</b>, is an <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_organization" title="Intergovernmental organization">intergovernmental</a> <a href="/wiki/Military_alliance" title="Military alliance">military alliance</a> between 30 <a href="/wiki/Member_states_of_NATO" title="Member states of NATO">member states</a> – 28 European and two North American. Established in the <a href="/wiki/Aftermath_of_World_War_II" title="Aftermath of World War II">aftermath of World War II</a>, the organization implements the <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Treaty" title="North Atlantic Treaty">North Atlantic Treaty</a>, which was signed in <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a>, on 4 April 1949.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> NATO is a system of <a href="/wiki/Collective_security" title="Collective security">collective security</a>: its independent member states agree to defend each other against attacks by third parties. During the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>, NATO operated as a check on the perceived threat posed by the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>. The alliance remained in place after the <a href="/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="Dissolution of the Soviet Union">dissolution of the Soviet Union</a> and has been involved in military operations in the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/NATO_headquarters" title="NATO headquarters">NATO's main headquarters</a> are located in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brussels,_Belgium" title="Brussels, Belgium">Brussels, Belgium</a>, while <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Headquarters_Allied_Powers_Europe" title="Supreme Headquarters Allied Powers Europe">NATO's military headquarters</a> are near <a href="/wiki/Mons" title="Mons">Mons</a>, Belgium. The alliance has targeted deployments of their <a href="/wiki/NATO_Response_Force" title="NATO Response Force">NATO Response Force</a> in Eastern Europe, and the combined militaries of all <a href="/wiki/Member_states_of_NATO" title="Member states of NATO">NATO members</a> include around 3.5 million soldiers and personnel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Their combined military spending as of 2020<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=NATO&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> constituted over 57 percent of <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_military_expenditures" title="List of countries by military expenditures">the global nominal total</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sipri1_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-sipri1-6">[6]</a></sup> Members have agreed that their aim is to reach or maintain the target defence spending of at least two percent of their <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">GDP</a> by 2024.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT_spending_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT_spending-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>NATO formed with <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Treaty#Founding_members" title="North Atlantic Treaty">twelve founding members</a>, and has <a href="/wiki/Enlargement_of_NATO" title="Enlargement of NATO">added new members</a> eight times, most recently when <a href="/wiki/North_Macedonia" title="North Macedonia">North Macedonia</a> joined the alliance in March 2020. Following the acceptance of their applications for membership in June 2022, <a href="/wiki/Finland%E2%80%93NATO_relations" title="Finland–NATO relations">Finland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sweden%E2%80%93NATO_relations" title="Sweden–NATO relations">Sweden</a> are anticipated to become the 31st and 32nd members, with their Accession Protocols to the North Atlantic Treaty now in the process of being ratified by the existing members.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> NATO currently also recognizes <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Accession_of_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina_to_NATO" title="Accession of Bosnia and Herzegovina to NATO">Bosnia and Herzegovina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georgia%E2%80%93NATO_relations" title="Georgia–NATO relations">Georgia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ukraine%E2%80%93NATO_relations" title="Ukraine–NATO relations">Ukraine</a> as aspiring members.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-3">[3]</a></sup> Enlargement has led to tensions with non-member <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, which is one of the twenty additional countries that participate in NATO's <a href="/wiki/Partnership_for_Peace" title="Partnership for Peace">Partnership for Peace</a> programme. Another nineteen countries are involved in institutionalized dialogue programmes with NATO.
</p>
<div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_NATO" title="History of NATO">History of NATO</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Dunkirk" title="Treaty of Dunkirk">Treaty of Dunkirk</a> was signed by <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> on 4 March 1947, during the <a href="/wiki/Aftermath_of_World_War_II" title="Aftermath of World War II">aftermath of World War II</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cold_War_(1947%E2%80%931953)" title="Cold War (1947–1953)">start of the Cold War</a>, as a <i>Treaty of Alliance and Mutual Assistance</i> in the event of possible attacks by <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>. In March 1948, this alliance was expanded in the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Brussels" title="Treaty of Brussels">Treaty of Brussels</a> to include the <a href="/wiki/Benelux" title="Benelux">Benelux</a> countries, forming the Brussels Treaty Organization, commonly known as the <a href="/wiki/Western_Union_(alliance)" title="Western Union (alliance)">Western Union</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Talks for a wider military alliance, which could include <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>, also began that month in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, where their foreign policy under the <a href="/wiki/Truman_Doctrine" title="Truman Doctrine">Truman Doctrine</a> promoted international solidarity against actions they saw as communist aggression, such as the <a href="/wiki/1948_Czechoslovak_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1948 Czechoslovak coup d'état">February 1948 coup d'état in Czechoslovakia</a>. These talks resulted in the signature of the <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Treaty" title="North Atlantic Treaty">North Atlantic Treaty</a> on 4 April 1949 by the member states of the Western Union plus the United States, Canada, Portugal, Italy, Norway, Denmark, and Iceland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-history-11">[11]</a></sup> Canadian diplomat <a href="/wiki/Lester_B._Pearson" title="Lester B. Pearson">Lester B. Pearson</a> was a key author and drafter of the treaty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-098967,_Aufnahme_der_Bundesrepublik_in_die_NATO.jpg"><img alt="A long rectangular room with multiple rows of seated individuals on each side, and flags hanging at the far end." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="596" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-098967%2C_Aufnahme_der_Bundesrepublik_in_die_NATO.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-098967%2C_Aufnahme_der_Bundesrepublik_in_die_NATO.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-098967%2C_Aufnahme_der_Bundesrepublik_in_die_NATO.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-098967%2C_Aufnahme_der_Bundesrepublik_in_die_NATO.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-098967%2C_Aufnahme_der_Bundesrepublik_in_die_NATO.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-098967%2C_Aufnahme_der_Bundesrepublik_in_die_NATO.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-098967,_Aufnahme_der_Bundesrepublik_in_die_NATO.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/West_Germany" title="West Germany">West Germany</a> joined NATO in 1955, which led to the formation of the rival <a href="/wiki/Warsaw_Pact" title="Warsaw Pact">Warsaw Pact</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The North Atlantic Treaty was largely dormant until the <a href="/wiki/Korean_War" title="Korean War">Korean War</a> initiated the establishment of NATO to implement it with an integrated military structure. This included the formation of <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Headquarters_Allied_Powers_Europe" title="Supreme Headquarters Allied Powers Europe">Supreme Headquarters Allied Powers Europe</a> (SHAPE) in 1951, which adopted many of the Western Union's military structures and plans,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pace_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-pace-15">[15]</a></sup> including their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standardization_Agreement" title="Standardization Agreement">agreements on standardizing equipment</a> and <a href="/wiki/Status_of_forces_agreement" title="Status of forces agreement">agreements on stationing foreign military forces</a> in European countries. In 1952, the post of <a href="/wiki/Secretary_General_of_NATO" title="Secretary General of NATO">Secretary General of NATO</a> was established as the organization's chief civilian. That year also saw the first major NATO <a href="/wiki/Military_exercise" title="Military exercise">maritime exercises</a>, <a href="/wiki/Exercise_Mainbrace" title="Exercise Mainbrace">Exercise Mainbrace</a> and the accession of <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a> and <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a> to the organization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Following the <a href="/wiki/London_and_Paris_Conferences" title="London and Paris Conferences">London and Paris Conferences</a>, <a href="/wiki/West_Germany" title="West Germany">West Germany</a> was permitted to rearm militarily, as they joined NATO in May 1955, which was, in turn, a major factor in the creation of the Soviet-dominated <a href="/wiki/Warsaw_Pact" title="Warsaw Pact">Warsaw Pact</a>, delineating the two opposing sides of the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>The building of the <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Wall" title="Berlin Wall">Berlin Wall</a> in 1961 marked a height in Cold War tensions, when 400,000 US troops were stationed in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Doubts over the strength of the relationship between the European states and the United States ebbed and flowed, along with doubts over the credibility of the NATO defence against a prospective <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet</a> invasion – doubts that led to the development of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Strike_Force_(France)" title="Strike Force (France)">independent French nuclear deterrent</a> and the withdrawal of <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> from NATO's military structure in 1966.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvan_der_Eyden2003104–106_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvan_der_Eyden2003104–106-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WP-France_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-WP-France-21">[21]</a></sup> In 1982, the newly democratic Spain joined the alliance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Revolutions_of_1989" title="Revolutions of 1989">Revolutions of 1989</a> in Europe led to a strategic re-evaluation of NATO's purpose, nature, tasks, and focus on the continent. In October 1990, <a href="/wiki/East_Germany" title="East Germany">East Germany</a> <a href="/wiki/German_reunification" title="German reunification">became part of the Federal Republic of Germany</a> and the alliance, and in November 1990, the alliance signed the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_on_Conventional_Armed_Forces_in_Europe" title="Treaty on Conventional Armed Forces in Europe">Treaty on Conventional Armed Forces in Europe</a> (CFE) in Paris with the Soviet Union. It mandated specific military reductions across the continent, which continued after the collapse of the <a href="/wiki/Warsaw_Pact" title="Warsaw Pact">Warsaw Pact</a> in February 1991 and the <a href="/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="Dissolution of the Soviet Union">dissolution of the Soviet Union</a> that December, which removed the <i>de facto</i> main adversaries of NATO.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> This began a draw-down of military spending and equipment in Europe. The CFE treaty allowed signatories to remove 52,000 pieces of conventional armaments in the following sixteen years,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> and allowed military spending by NATO's European members to decline by 28 percent from 1990 to 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> In 1990 assurances were given by several Western leaders to <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Gorbachev" title="Mikhail Gorbachev">Mikhail Gorbachev</a> that NATO would not expand further east, as revealed by memoranda of private conversations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> However, the final text of the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_on_the_Final_Settlement_with_Respect_to_Germany" title="Treaty on the Final Settlement with Respect to Germany">Treaty on the Final Settlement with Respect to Germany</a>, signed later that year, contained no mention of the issue of eastward expansion.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Berlin_Wall_at_NATO_Headquarters.jpg"><img alt="Two tall concrete sections of a wall in a grass lawn in front of a curved building with blue-glass windows." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Berlin_Wall_at_NATO_Headquarters.jpg/220px-Berlin_Wall_at_NATO_Headquarters.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Berlin_Wall_at_NATO_Headquarters.jpg/330px-Berlin_Wall_at_NATO_Headquarters.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Berlin_Wall_at_NATO_Headquarters.jpg/440px-Berlin_Wall_at_NATO_Headquarters.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Berlin_Wall_at_NATO_Headquarters.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_the_Berlin_Wall" title="Fall of the Berlin Wall">Fall of the Berlin Wall</a> in 1989 marked a turning point in NATO's role in Europe, and this section of the wall is now displayed outside NATO Headquarters.</div></div></div>
<p>In the 1990s, the organization extended its activities into political and humanitarian situations that had not formerly been NATO concerns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rfe_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-rfe-30">[30]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Breakup_of_Yugoslavia" title="Breakup of Yugoslavia">Breakup of Yugoslavia</a>, the organization conducted its first <a href="/wiki/List_of_NATO_operations" title="List of NATO operations">military interventions</a> in <a href="/wiki/NATO_intervention_in_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="NATO intervention in Bosnia and Herzegovina">Bosnia from 1992 to 1995</a> and later <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1999_NATO_bombing_of_Yugoslavia" title="1999 NATO bombing of Yugoslavia">Yugoslavia in 1999</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> These conflicts motivated a major post-Cold War military restructuring. NATO's military structure was cut back and reorganized, with new forces such as the <a href="/wiki/Allied_Rapid_Reaction_Corps" title="Allied Rapid Reaction Corps">Headquarters Allied Command Europe Rapid Reaction Corps</a> established.
</p><p>Politically, the organization sought better relations with the newly autonomous <a href="/wiki/Central_and_Eastern_Europe" title="Central and Eastern Europe">Central and Eastern European</a> nations, and diplomatic forums for regional cooperation between NATO and its neighbours were set up during this post-Cold War period, including the <a href="/wiki/Partnership_for_Peace" title="Partnership for Peace">Partnership for Peace</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Dialogue" title="Mediterranean Dialogue">Mediterranean Dialogue</a> initiative in 1994, the <a href="/wiki/Euro-Atlantic_Partnership_Council" title="Euro-Atlantic Partnership Council">Euro-Atlantic Partnership Council</a> in 1997, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NATO%E2%80%93Russia_relations" title="NATO–Russia relations">NATO–Russia Permanent Joint Council</a> in 1998. At the <a href="/wiki/1999_Washington_summit" title="1999 Washington summit">1999 Washington summit</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungary</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech Republic</a> officially joined NATO, and the organization also issued new guidelines for membership with individualized "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Membership_Action_Plan" title="Membership Action Plan">Membership Action Plans</a>". These plans governed the addition of new alliance members: <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Latvia" title="Latvia">Latvia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lithuania" title="Lithuania">Lithuania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Slovakia" title="Slovakia">Slovakia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Slovenia" title="Slovenia">Slovenia</a> in 2004, <a href="/wiki/Albania" title="Albania">Albania</a> and <a href="/wiki/Croatia" title="Croatia">Croatia</a> in 2009, <a href="/wiki/Montenegro" title="Montenegro">Montenegro</a> in 2017, and <a href="/wiki/North_Macedonia" title="North Macedonia">North Macedonia</a> in 2020.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> The election of French President <a href="/wiki/Nicolas_Sarkozy" title="Nicolas Sarkozy">Nicolas Sarkozy</a> in 2007 led to a major reform of France's military position, culminating with the return to full membership on 4 April 2009, which also included France rejoining the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NATO_Military_Command_Structure" title="NATO Military Command Structure">NATO Military Command Structure</a>, while maintaining an independent nuclear deterrent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WP-France_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-WP-France-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guardian-france_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-guardian-france-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DPC_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-DPC-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/North_Atlantic_Treaty#Article_5" title="s:North Atlantic Treaty">Article 5 of the North Atlantic treaty</a>, requiring member states to come to the aid of any member state subject to an armed attack, was invoked for the first and only time after the <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">September 11 attacks</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> after which troops were deployed to <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a> under the NATO-led <a href="/wiki/International_Security_Assistance_Force" title="International Security Assistance Force">ISAF</a>. The organization has operated a range of additional roles since then, including sending <a href="/wiki/NATO_Training_Mission_%E2%80%93_Iraq" title="NATO Training Mission – Iraq">trainers to Iraq</a>, assisting in <a href="/wiki/Operation_Ocean_Shield" title="Operation Ocean Shield">counter-piracy operations</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> and in 2011 enforcing a <a href="/wiki/No-fly_zone" title="No-fly zone">no-fly zone</a> <a href="/wiki/2011_military_intervention_in_Libya" title="2011 military intervention in Libya">over Libya</a> in accordance with <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council_Resolution_1973" title="United Nations Security Council Resolution 1973">UN Security Council Resolution 1973</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Annexation_of_Crimea_by_the_Russian_Federation" title="Annexation of Crimea by the Russian Federation">Russia's annexation of Crimea</a> led to strong condemnation by NATO nations,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> and was one of the seven times that <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Treaty#Article_4" title="North Atlantic Treaty">Article 4</a>, which calls for consultation among NATO members, has been invoked. Prior times included during the <a href="/wiki/Iraq_War" title="Iraq War">Iraq War</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syrian_Civil_War" title="Syrian Civil War">Syrian Civil War</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> At the <a href="/wiki/2014_Wales_summit" title="2014 Wales summit">2014 Wales summit</a>, the leaders of NATO's member states formally committed for the first time to spend the equivalent of at least two percent of their <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">gross domestic products</a> on defence by 2024, which had previously been only an informal guideline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> At the <a href="/wiki/2016_Warsaw_summit" title="2016 Warsaw summit">2016 Warsaw summit</a>, NATO countries agreed on the creation of <a href="/wiki/NATO_Enhanced_Forward_Presence" title="NATO Enhanced Forward Presence">NATO Enhanced Forward Presence</a>, which deployed four multinational battalion-sized battlegroups in Estonia, Latvia, Lithuania, and Poland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EFP_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-EFP-40">[40]</a></sup> Before and during the <a href="/wiki/2022_Russian_invasion_of_Ukraine" title="2022 Russian invasion of Ukraine">2022 Russian invasion of Ukraine</a>, several NATO countries sent ground troops, warships and fighter aircraft to reinforce the alliance's eastern flank, and multiple countries again invoked Article 4.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> In March 2022, NATO leaders met at Brussels for an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2022_Brussels_extraordinary_summit" title="2022 Brussels extraordinary summit">extraordinary summit</a> which also involved <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Group_of_Seven" title="Group of Seven">Group of Seven</a> and <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> leaders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> NATO member states agreed to establish four additional battlegroups in Bulgaria, Hungary, Romania, and Slovakia,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EFP_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-EFP-40">[40]</a></sup> and elements of the <a href="/wiki/NATO_Response_Force" title="NATO Response Force">NATO Response Force</a> were activated for the first time in NATO's history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>As of June 2022, NATO had deployed 40,000 troops along its 2,500-kilometres-long Eastern flank to deter Russian aggression. More than half of this number have been deployed in Bulgaria, Romania, Hungary, Slovakia, and Poland, which five countries muster a considerable combined ex-NATO force of 259,000 troops. To supplement Bulgaria's Air Force, Spain sent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eurofighter_Typhoons" title="Eurofighter Typhoons">Eurofighter Typhoons</a>, the Netherlands sent eight <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/F-35" title="F-35">F-35</a> attack aircraft, and additional French and US attack aircraft would arrive soon as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-birn14.6_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-birn14.6-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>NATO enjoys public support across its member states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Military_operations">Military operations</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_NATO_operations" title="List of NATO operations">List of NATO operations</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_operations">Early operations</span></h3>
<p>No military operations were conducted by NATO during the Cold War. Following the end of the Cold War, the first operations, <i>Anchor Guard</i> in 1990 and <i>Ace Guard</i> in 1991, were prompted by the Iraqi <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Kuwait" title="Invasion of Kuwait">invasion of Kuwait</a>. Airborne early warning aircraft were sent to provide coverage of southeastern Turkey, and later a quick-reaction force was deployed to the area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NATO_Operations_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-NATO_Operations-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bosnia_and_Herzegovina_intervention">Bosnia and Herzegovina intervention</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/NATO_intervention_in_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="NATO intervention in Bosnia and Herzegovina">NATO intervention in Bosnia and Herzegovina</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:F-16_deliberate_force.JPG"><img alt="A fighter jet with AV marked on its tail takes off from a mountain runway." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="416" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/F-16_deliberate_force.JPG/220px-F-16_deliberate_force.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/F-16_deliberate_force.JPG/330px-F-16_deliberate_force.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/F-16_deliberate_force.JPG/440px-F-16_deliberate_force.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:F-16_deliberate_force.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>NATO planes engaging in aerial bombardments during <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1995_NATO_bombing_campaign_in_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="1995 NATO bombing campaign in Bosnia and Herzegovina">Operation Deliberate Force</a> after the <a href="/wiki/Srebrenica_massacre" title="Srebrenica massacre">Srebrenica massacre</a></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Bosnian_War" title="Bosnian War">Bosnian War</a> began in 1992, as a result of the <a href="/wiki/Breakup_of_Yugoslavia" title="Breakup of Yugoslavia">Breakup of Yugoslavia</a>. The deteriorating situation led to <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council_Resolution_816" title="United Nations Security Council Resolution 816">United Nations Security Council Resolution 816</a> on 9 October 1992, ordering a <a href="/wiki/No-fly_zone" title="No-fly zone">no-fly zone</a> over central Bosnia and Herzegovina, which NATO began enforcing on 12 April 1993 with <a href="/wiki/Operation_Deny_Flight" title="Operation Deny Flight">Operation Deny Flight</a>. From June 1993 until October 1996, <a href="/wiki/Operation_Sharp_Guard" title="Operation Sharp Guard">Operation Sharp Guard</a> added maritime enforcement of the <a href="/wiki/Arms_embargo" title="Arms embargo">arms embargo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Economic_sanctions" title="Economic sanctions">economic sanctions</a> against the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Federal_Republic_of_Yugoslavia" title="Federal Republic of Yugoslavia">Federal Republic of Yugoslavia</a>. On 28 February 1994, NATO took its first wartime action by <a href="/wiki/Banja_Luka_incident" title="Banja Luka incident">shooting down four Bosnian Serb aircraft</a> violating the no-fly zone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZenko2010133–134_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZenko2010133–134-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 10 and 11 April 1994, the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Protection_Force" title="United Nations Protection Force">United Nations Protection Force</a> called in air strikes to protect the <a href="/wiki/Gora%C5%BEde" title="Goražde">Goražde</a> safe area, resulting in the bombing of a Bosnian Serb military command outpost near Goražde by two US <a href="/wiki/General_Dynamics_F-16_Fighting_Falcon" title="General Dynamics F-16 Fighting Falcon">F-16</a> jets acting under NATO direction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZenko2010134_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZenko2010134-50">[50]</a></sup> In retaliation, Serbs took 150 U.N. personnel hostage on 14 April.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> On 16 April a British <a href="/wiki/British_Aerospace_Sea_Harrier" title="British Aerospace Sea Harrier">Sea Harrier</a> was shot down over Goražde by Serb forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBethlehemWeller1997liiv_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBethlehemWeller1997liiv-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>In August 1995, a two-week NATO bombing campaign, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1995_NATO_bombing_campaign_in_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="1995 NATO bombing campaign in Bosnia and Herzegovina">Operation Deliberate Force</a>, began against the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Army_of_the_Republika_Srpska" title="Army of the Republika Srpska">Army of the Republika Srpska</a>, after the <a href="/wiki/Srebrenica_massacre" title="Srebrenica massacre">Srebrenica genocide</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zenko137_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-zenko137-54">[54]</a></sup> Further NATO air strikes helped bring the <a href="/wiki/Yugoslav_Wars" title="Yugoslav Wars">Yugoslav Wars</a> to an end, resulting in the <a href="/wiki/Dayton_Agreement" title="Dayton Agreement">Dayton Agreement</a> in November 1995.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zenko137_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-zenko137-54">[54]</a></sup> As part of this agreement, NATO deployed a UN-mandated peacekeeping force, under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Operation_Joint_Endeavor" title="Operation Joint Endeavor">Operation Joint Endeavor</a>, named <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IFOR" title="IFOR">IFOR</a>. Almost 60,000 NATO troops were joined by forces from non-NATO nations in this peacekeeping mission. This transitioned into the smaller <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SFOR" title="SFOR">SFOR</a>, which started with 32,000 troops initially and ran from December 1996 until December 2004, when operations were then passed onto <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/EUFOR_Althea" title="EUFOR Althea">European Union Force Althea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClausson200694–97_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClausson200694–97-55">[55]</a></sup> Following the lead of its member nations, NATO began to award a service medal, the <a href="/wiki/NATO_Medal" title="NATO Medal">NATO Medal</a>, for these operations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kosovo_intervention">Kosovo intervention</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1999_NATO_bombing_of_Yugoslavia" title="1999 NATO bombing of Yugoslavia">1999 NATO bombing of Yugoslavia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kosovo_Force" title="Kosovo Force">KFOR</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:German_KFOR_troops_patrol_southern_Kosovo,_summer_1999.jpg"><img alt="Three trucks of soldiers idle on a country road in front of trees and red-roofed houses. The rear truck has KFOR painted on is back." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2280" decoding="async" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/German_KFOR_troops_patrol_southern_Kosovo%2C_summer_1999.jpg/220px-German_KFOR_troops_patrol_southern_Kosovo%2C_summer_1999.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/German_KFOR_troops_patrol_southern_Kosovo%2C_summer_1999.jpg/330px-German_KFOR_troops_patrol_southern_Kosovo%2C_summer_1999.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/German_KFOR_troops_patrol_southern_Kosovo%2C_summer_1999.jpg/440px-German_KFOR_troops_patrol_southern_Kosovo%2C_summer_1999.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:German_KFOR_troops_patrol_southern_Kosovo,_summer_1999.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>German <a href="/wiki/Kosovo_Force" title="Kosovo Force">KFOR</a> soldiers on patrol in southern Kosovo in 1999</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MSU_Mitrovica_Manbox_-_Ibar_Bridge_summer_2019.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2033" data-file-width="4160" decoding="async" height="108" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/MSU_Mitrovica_Manbox_-_Ibar_Bridge_summer_2019.jpg/220px-MSU_Mitrovica_Manbox_-_Ibar_Bridge_summer_2019.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/MSU_Mitrovica_Manbox_-_Ibar_Bridge_summer_2019.jpg/330px-MSU_Mitrovica_Manbox_-_Ibar_Bridge_summer_2019.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/MSU_Mitrovica_Manbox_-_Ibar_Bridge_summer_2019.jpg/440px-MSU_Mitrovica_Manbox_-_Ibar_Bridge_summer_2019.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MSU_Mitrovica_Manbox_-_Ibar_Bridge_summer_2019.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Kosovo_Force" title="Kosovo Force">KFOR</a>-<a href="/wiki/Multinational_Specialized_Unit" title="Multinational Specialized Unit">MSU</a> <a href="/wiki/Carabinieri" title="Carabinieri">Carabinieri</a> Patrols in front of the <a href="/wiki/New_Bridge,_Mitrovica" title="New Bridge, Mitrovica">Ibar Bridge</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mitrovica,_Kosovo" title="Mitrovica, Kosovo">Mitrovica, Kosovo</a>, 2019</div></div></div>
<p>In an effort to stop <a href="/wiki/Slobodan_Milo%C5%A1evi%C4%87" title="Slobodan Milošević">Slobodan Milošević</a>'s Serbian-led crackdown on <a href="/wiki/Kosovo_Liberation_Army" title="Kosovo Liberation Army">KLA</a> separatists and Albanian civilians in <a href="/wiki/Kosovo" title="Kosovo">Kosovo</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council" title="United Nations Security Council">United Nations Security Council</a> passed <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council_Resolution_1199" title="United Nations Security Council Resolution 1199">Resolution 1199</a> on 23 September 1998 to demand a ceasefire. Negotiations under US Special Envoy <a href="/wiki/Richard_Holbrooke" title="Richard Holbrooke">Richard Holbrooke</a> broke down on 23 March 1999, and he handed the matter to NATO,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> which started a 78-day bombing campaign on 24 March 1999.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Operation Allied Force targeted the military capabilities of what was then the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Federal_Republic_of_Yugoslavia" title="Federal Republic of Yugoslavia">Federal Republic of Yugoslavia</a>. During the crisis, NATO also deployed one of its international reaction forces, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ACE_Mobile_Force_(Land)" title="ACE Mobile Force (Land)">ACE Mobile Force (Land)</a>, to Albania as the Albania Force (AFOR), to deliver humanitarian aid to refugees from Kosovo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gs_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-gs-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>Though the campaign was criticized for <a href="/wiki/Civilian_casualties_during_Operation_Allied_Force" title="Civilian casualties during Operation Allied Force">high civilian casualties</a>, including bombing of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._bombing_of_the_Chinese_embassy_in_Belgrade" title="U.S. bombing of the Chinese embassy in Belgrade">Chinese embassy in Belgrade</a>, Milošević finally accepted the terms of an international peace plan on 3 June 1999, ending the <a href="/wiki/Kosovo_War" title="Kosovo War">Kosovo War</a>. On 11 June, Milošević further accepted <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council_Resolution_1244" title="United Nations Security Council Resolution 1244">UN resolution 1244</a>, under the mandate of which NATO then helped establish the <a href="/wiki/Kosovo_Force" title="Kosovo Force">KFOR</a> peacekeeping force. Nearly one million refugees had fled Kosovo, and part of KFOR's mandate was to protect the humanitarian missions, in addition to deterring violence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gs_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-gs-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> In August–September 2001, the alliance also mounted <a href="/wiki/Operation_Essential_Harvest" title="Operation Essential Harvest">Operation Essential Harvest</a>, a mission disarming ethnic Albanian militias in the Republic of Macedonia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> As of 1 December 2013<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=NATO&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, 4,882 KFOR soldiers, representing 31 countries, continue to operate in the area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research#Primary,_secondary_and_tertiary_sources" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable secondary sources. (April 2022)">non-primary source needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The US, the UK, and most other NATO countries opposed efforts to require the UN Security Council to approve NATO military strikes, such as the action against Serbia in 1999, while France and some others claimed that the alliance needed UN approval.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> The US/UK side claimed that this would undermine the authority of the alliance, and they noted that Russia and China would have exercised their Security Council vetoes to block the strike on <a href="/wiki/Yugoslavia" title="Yugoslavia">Yugoslavia</a>, and could do the same in future conflicts where NATO intervention was required, thus nullifying the entire potency and purpose of the organization. Recognizing the post-Cold War military environment, NATO adopted the Alliance Strategic Concept during its <a href="/wiki/1999_Washington_summit" title="1999 Washington summit">Washington summit</a> in April 1999 that emphasized conflict prevention and crisis management.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nato.int_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-nato.int-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research#Primary,_secondary_and_tertiary_sources" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable secondary sources. (April 2022)">non-primary source needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="War_in_Afghanistan">War in Afghanistan</span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/International_Security_Assistance_Force" title="International Security Assistance Force">International Security Assistance Force</a> and <a href="/wiki/War_in_Afghanistan_(2001%E2%80%932021)" title="War in Afghanistan (2001–2021)">War in Afghanistan</a></div><div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:National_Park_Service_9-11_Statue_of_Liberty_and_WTC.jpg"><img alt="A monumental green copper statue of a woman with a torch stands on an island in front of a mainland where a massive plume of grey smoke billows among skyscrapers." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="315" data-file-width="385" decoding="async" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/National_Park_Service_9-11_Statue_of_Liberty_and_WTC.jpg/220px-National_Park_Service_9-11_Statue_of_Liberty_and_WTC.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/National_Park_Service_9-11_Statue_of_Liberty_and_WTC.jpg/330px-National_Park_Service_9-11_Statue_of_Liberty_and_WTC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2f/National_Park_Service_9-11_Statue_of_Liberty_and_WTC.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:National_Park_Service_9-11_Statue_of_Liberty_and_WTC.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">September 11 attacks</a> in the United States caused NATO to invoke its collective defence article for the first time.</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">September 11 attacks</a> in the United States caused NATO to invoke <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NATO_Article_5" title="NATO Article 5">Article 5</a> of the NATO Charter for the first time in the organization's history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> The Article states that an attack on any member shall be considered to be an attack on all. The invocation was confirmed on 4 October 2001 when NATO determined that the attacks were indeed eligible under the terms of the North Atlantic Treaty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research#Primary,_secondary_and_tertiary_sources" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable secondary sources. (April 2022)">non-primary source needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The eight official actions taken by NATO in response to the attacks included <a href="/wiki/Operation_Eagle_Assist" title="Operation Eagle Assist">Operation Eagle Assist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Operation_Active_Endeavour" title="Operation Active Endeavour">Operation Active Endeavour</a>, a naval operation in the Mediterranean Sea designed to prevent the movement of terrorists or weapons of mass destruction, and to enhance the security of shipping in general, which began on 4 October 2001.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research#Primary,_secondary_and_tertiary_sources" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable secondary sources. (April 2022)">non-primary source needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The alliance showed unity: on 16 April 2003, NATO agreed to take command of the <a href="/wiki/International_Security_Assistance_Force" title="International Security Assistance Force">International Security Assistance Force</a> (ISAF), which included troops from 42 countries. The decision came at the request of Germany and the Netherlands, the two nations leading ISAF at the time of the agreement, and all nineteen NATO ambassadors approved it unanimously. The handover of control to NATO took place on 11 August, and marked the first time in NATO's history that it took charge of a mission outside the north Atlantic area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (April 2022)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:210321-D-BN624-1005_(51058690203).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4024" data-file-width="6048" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/210321-D-BN624-1005_%2851058690203%29.jpg/220px-210321-D-BN624-1005_%2851058690203%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/210321-D-BN624-1005_%2851058690203%29.jpg/330px-210321-D-BN624-1005_%2851058690203%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/210321-D-BN624-1005_%2851058690203%29.jpg/440px-210321-D-BN624-1005_%2851058690203%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:210321-D-BN624-1005_(51058690203).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>General <a href="/wiki/Austin_S._Miller" title="Austin S. Miller">Austin S. Miller</a> (right) became commander of U.S. and <a href="/wiki/Resolute_Support_Mission" title="Resolute Support Mission">NATO forces</a> in Afghanistan in September 2018 and oversaw the <a href="/wiki/Withdrawal_of_United_States_troops_from_Afghanistan_(2020%E2%80%932021)" title="Withdrawal of United States troops from Afghanistan (2020–2021)">withdrawal</a> until July 2021. The 28th <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_Defense" title="United States Secretary of Defense">United States Secretary of Defense</a> is on the left.</div></div></div>
<p>ISAF was initially charged with securing <a href="/wiki/Kabul" title="Kabul">Kabul</a> and surrounding areas from the <a href="/wiki/Taliban" title="Taliban">Taliban</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al_Qaeda" title="Al Qaeda">al Qaeda</a> and factional warlords, so as to allow for the establishment of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Afghan_Transitional_Administration" title="Afghan Transitional Administration">Afghan Transitional Administration</a> headed by <a href="/wiki/Hamid_Karzai" title="Hamid Karzai">Hamid Karzai</a>. In October 2003, the UN Security Council authorized the expansion of the ISAF mission throughout Afghanistan,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research#Primary,_secondary_and_tertiary_sources" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable secondary sources. (April 2022)">non-primary source needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and ISAF subsequently expanded the mission in four main stages over the whole of the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ISAF_Chronology_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-ISAF_Chronology-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research#Primary,_secondary_and_tertiary_sources" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable secondary sources. (April 2022)">non-primary source needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>On 31 July 2006, the ISAF additionally took over <a href="/wiki/Coalition_combat_operations_in_Afghanistan_in_2006" title="Coalition combat operations in Afghanistan in 2006">military operations in the south of Afghanistan</a> from a US-led anti-terrorism coalition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Due to the intensity of the fighting in the south, in 2011 France allowed a squadron of <a href="/wiki/Dassault_Mirage_2000" title="Dassault Mirage 2000">Mirage 2000</a> fighter/attack aircraft to be moved into the area, to <a href="/wiki/Kandahar_International_Airport" title="Kandahar International Airport">Kandahar</a>, in order to reinforce the alliance's efforts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> During its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2012_Chicago_Summit" title="2012 Chicago Summit">2012 Chicago Summit</a>, NATO endorsed a plan to end the Afghanistan war and to remove the NATO-led ISAF Forces by the end of December 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reuters_20120521_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters_20120521-73">[73]</a></sup> ISAF was disestablished in December 2014 and replaced by the follow-on training <a href="/wiki/Resolute_Support_Mission" title="Resolute Support Mission">Resolute Support Mission</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 14 April 2021, NATO Secretary General <a href="/wiki/Jens_Stoltenberg" title="Jens Stoltenberg">Jens Stoltenberg</a> said the alliance had agreed to start <a href="/wiki/Withdrawal_of_United_States_troops_from_Afghanistan_(2020%E2%80%932021)" title="Withdrawal of United States troops from Afghanistan (2020–2021)">withdrawing its troops from Afghanistan</a> by May 1.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> Soon after the withdrawal of NATO troops started, the Taliban launched an <a href="/wiki/2021_Taliban_offensive" title="2021 Taliban offensive">offensive against the Afghan government</a>, quickly advancing in front of collapsing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Afghan_Armed_Forces" title="Afghan Armed Forces">Afghan Armed Forces</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> By 15 August 2021, Taliban militants controlled the vast majority of Afghanistan and had encircled the capital city of <a href="/wiki/Kabul" title="Kabul">Kabul</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> Some politicians in NATO member states have described the chaotic withdrawal of Western troops from Afghanistan and the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Kabul_(2021)" title="Fall of Kabul (2021)">collapse of the Afghan government</a> as the greatest debacle that NATO has suffered since its founding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Iraq_training_mission">Iraq training mission</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Armentani_and_Trujillo.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1764" data-file-width="2425" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Armentani_and_Trujillo.jpg/220px-Armentani_and_Trujillo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Armentani_and_Trujillo.jpg/330px-Armentani_and_Trujillo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Armentani_and_Trujillo.jpg/440px-Armentani_and_Trujillo.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Armentani_and_Trujillo.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Italian Major General Giovanni Armentani, Deputy Commanding General for the NATO Training Mission, meets with a U.S. Advise and Assist Brigade.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/NATO_Training_Mission_%E2%80%93_Iraq" title="NATO Training Mission – Iraq">NATO Training Mission – Iraq</a></div>
<p>In August 2004, during the <a href="/wiki/Iraq_War" title="Iraq War">Iraq War</a>, NATO formed the <a href="/wiki/NATO_Training_Mission_%E2%80%93_Iraq" title="NATO Training Mission – Iraq">NATO Training Mission – Iraq</a>, a training mission to assist the Iraqi security forces in conjunction with the US-led <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multinational_Force_%E2%80%93_Iraq" title="Multinational Force – Iraq">MNF-I</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> The NATO Training Mission-Iraq (NTM-I) was established at the request of the <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Interim_Government" title="Iraqi Interim Government">Iraqi Interim Government</a> under the provisions of <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council_Resolution_1546" title="United Nations Security Council Resolution 1546">United Nations Security Council Resolution 1546</a>. The aim of NTM-I was to assist in the development of Iraqi security forces training structures and institutions so that Iraq can build an effective and sustainable capability that addresses the needs of the nation. NTM-I was not a combat mission but is a distinct mission, under the political control of the <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Council" title="North Atlantic Council">North Atlantic Council</a>. Its operational emphasis was on training and mentoring. The activities of the mission were coordinated with Iraqi authorities and the US-led Deputy Commanding General Advising and Training, who was also dual-hatted as the Commander of NTM-I. The mission officially concluded on 17 December 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a> invoked the first Article 4 meetings in 2003 at the start of the <a href="/wiki/Iraq_War" title="Iraq War">Iraq War</a>. Turkey also invoked this article twice in 2012 during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syrian_Civil_War" title="Syrian Civil War">Syrian Civil War</a>, after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/June_2012_interception_of_Turkish_aircraft" title="June 2012 interception of Turkish aircraft">downing</a> of an unarmed Turkish <a href="/wiki/McDonnell_Douglas_F-4_Phantom_II" title="McDonnell Douglas F-4 Phantom II">F-4 reconnaissance jet</a>, and after a mortar was fired at Turkey from <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> and again in 2015 after threats by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_State_of_Iraq_and_the_Levant" title="Islamic State of Iraq and the Levant">Islamic State of Iraq and the Levant</a> to its territorial integrity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gulf_of_Aden_anti-piracy">Gulf of Aden anti-piracy</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gulf_of_Aden_-_disabled_pirate_boat.jpg"><img alt="A tall plume of black smoke rises from the blue ocean waters next to a large grey battleship and a small black inflatable boat." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2832" data-file-width="4256" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Gulf_of_Aden_-_disabled_pirate_boat.jpg/220px-Gulf_of_Aden_-_disabled_pirate_boat.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Gulf_of_Aden_-_disabled_pirate_boat.jpg/330px-Gulf_of_Aden_-_disabled_pirate_boat.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Gulf_of_Aden_-_disabled_pirate_boat.jpg/440px-Gulf_of_Aden_-_disabled_pirate_boat.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gulf_of_Aden_-_disabled_pirate_boat.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/USS_Farragut_(DDG-99)" title="USS Farragut (DDG-99)">USS <i>Farragut</i></a> destroying a Somali pirate skiff in March 2010</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Operation_Ocean_Shield" title="Operation Ocean Shield">Operation Ocean Shield</a></div>
<p>Beginning on 17 August 2009, NATO deployed warships in an operation to protect maritime traffic in the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Aden" title="Gulf of Aden">Gulf of Aden</a> and the Indian Ocean from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Piracy_in_Somalia" title="Piracy in Somalia">Somali pirates</a>, and help strengthen the navies and coast guards of regional states. The operation was approved by the <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Council" title="North Atlantic Council">North Atlantic Council</a> and involved warships primarily from the United States though vessels from many other nations were also included. Operation Ocean Shield focused on protecting the ships of Operation Allied Provider which were distributing aid as part of the <a href="/wiki/World_Food_Programme" title="World Food Programme">World Food Programme</a> mission in Somalia. Russia, China and South Korea sent warships to participate in the activities as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research#Primary,_secondary_and_tertiary_sources" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable secondary sources. (April 2022)">non-primary source needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research#Primary,_secondary_and_tertiary_sources" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable secondary sources. (April 2022)">non-primary source needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The operation sought to dissuade and interrupt pirate attacks, protect vessels, and to increase the general level of security in the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research#Primary,_secondary_and_tertiary_sources" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable secondary sources. (April 2022)">non-primary source needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Libya_intervention">Libya intervention</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/2011_military_intervention_in_Libya" title="2011 military intervention in Libya">2011 military intervention in Libya</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Palmaria_bengasi_1903_0612_b1.jpg"><img alt="Pieces of a destroyed tank, notably the gun turret, lie on a sandy landscape." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="963" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Palmaria_bengasi_1903_0612_b1.jpg/220px-Palmaria_bengasi_1903_0612_b1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Palmaria_bengasi_1903_0612_b1.jpg/330px-Palmaria_bengasi_1903_0612_b1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Palmaria_bengasi_1903_0612_b1.jpg/440px-Palmaria_bengasi_1903_0612_b1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Palmaria_bengasi_1903_0612_b1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Libyan Army <a href="/wiki/Palmaria_(artillery)" title="Palmaria (artillery)">Palmaria howitzers</a> destroyed by the <a href="/wiki/French_Air_and_Space_Force" title="French Air and Space Force">French Air Force</a> near <a href="/wiki/Benghazi" title="Benghazi">Benghazi</a> in March 2011</div></div></div>
<p>During the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Libyan_Civil_War_(2011)" title="Libyan Civil War (2011)">Libyan Civil War</a>, violence between protesters and the Libyan government under Colonel <a href="/wiki/Muammar_Gaddafi" title="Muammar Gaddafi">Muammar Gaddafi</a> escalated, and on 17 March 2011 led to the passage of <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council_Resolution_1973" title="United Nations Security Council Resolution 1973">United Nations Security Council Resolution 1973</a>, which called for a ceasefire, and authorized military action to protect civilians. A coalition that included several NATO members began enforcing a no-fly zone over Libya shortly afterwards, beginning with <a href="/wiki/Op%C3%A9ration_Harmattan" title="Opération Harmattan">Opération Harmattan</a> by the <a href="/wiki/French_Air_and_Space_Force" title="French Air and Space Force">French Air Force</a> on 19 March.
</p><p>On 20 March 2011, NATO states agreed on enforcing an arms embargo against Libya with <a href="/wiki/Operation_Unified_Protector" title="Operation Unified Protector">Operation Unified Protector</a> using ships from NATO <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standing_NRF_Maritime_Group_1" title="Standing NRF Maritime Group 1">Standing Maritime Group 1</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standing_NRF_Mine_Countermeasures_Group_1" title="Standing NRF Mine Countermeasures Group 1">Standing Mine Countermeasures Group 1</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NATOSecGen1_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-NATOSecGen1-87">[87]</a></sup> and additional ships and submarines from NATO members.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NATOPressBriefing1_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-NATOPressBriefing1-88">[88]</a></sup> They would "monitor, report and, if needed, <a href="/wiki/Interdiction" title="Interdiction">interdict</a> vessels suspected of carrying illegal arms or <a href="/wiki/Mercenary" title="Mercenary">mercenaries</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NATOSecGen1_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-NATOSecGen1-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 24 March, NATO agreed to take control of the no-fly zone from the initial coalition, while command of targeting ground units remained with the coalition's forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> NATO began officially enforcing the UN resolution on 27 March 2011 with assistance from <a href="/wiki/Qatar" title="Qatar">Qatar</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_Arab_Emirates" title="United Arab Emirates">United Arab Emirates</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> By June, reports of divisions within the alliance surfaced as only eight of the 28 member nations were participating in combat operations,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> resulting in a confrontation between US Defense Secretary <a href="/wiki/Robert_Gates" title="Robert Gates">Robert Gates</a> and countries such as Poland, Spain, the Netherlands, Turkey, and Germany with Gates calling on the latter to contribute more and the latter believing the organization has overstepped its mandate in the conflict.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> In his final policy speech in Brussels on 10 June, Gates further criticized allied countries in suggesting their actions could cause the demise of NATO.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> The German foreign ministry pointed to "a considerable [German] contribution to NATO and NATO-led operations" and to the fact that this engagement was highly valued by President Obama.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p>While the mission was extended into September, <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a> that day (10 June) announced it would begin scaling down contributions and complete withdrawal by 1 August.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> Earlier that week it was reported <a href="/wiki/Royal_Danish_Air_Force" title="Royal Danish Air Force">Danish air fighters</a> were running out of bombs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> The following week, the head of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a> said the country's operations in the conflict were not sustainable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> By the end of the mission in October 2011, after the death of Colonel Gaddafi, NATO planes had flown about 9,500 strike sorties against pro-Gaddafi targets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> A report from the organization <a href="/wiki/Human_Rights_Watch" title="Human Rights Watch">Human Rights Watch</a> in May 2012 identified at least 72 civilians killed in the campaign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup>
</p><p>Following a <a href="/wiki/2013_Libyan_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat_attempt" title="2013 Libyan coup d'état attempt">coup d'état attempt</a> in October 2013, Libyan Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Ali_Zeidan" title="Ali Zeidan">Ali Zeidan</a> requested technical advice and trainers from NATO to assist with ongoing security issues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Membership">Membership</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Member_states_of_NATO" title="Member states of NATO">Member states of NATO</a></div>
